The registered office is a serviced suite in a Nicosia office block — the kind of address that exists on paper and nowhere else. The company has no employees, no assets, no website, no physical presence. It exists for one purpose: to create a legal wall between Prometheus and whatever it moves."

"That's professional."

"That's intelligence-grade tradecraft. The kind of corporate structure that takes lawyers and accountants to build and forensic analysts to unravel. Prometheus didn't set this up last week. They've been preparing the infrastructure for months. Maybe years."

"Rathore—"

"There's more. INTERPOL's Cultural Heritage unit has been tracking a pattern that I think is connected. Three thefts in the last six months, all targeting materials that match the Ashokan profile. London, three months ago: a collection of Mughal-era manuscripts stolen from a private home in Mayfair. The thieves bypassed the alarm system, entered through a rear window, and took only the Indian materials — leaving behind jewelry, paintings, silver, and antique watches worth considerably more than the manuscripts' market value. Berlin, six months ago: a Brahmi-inscribed copper plate taken from the Museum of Asian Art during a power outage that lasted exactly eleven minutes. The power failure was caused by a localized electrical surge that investigators later determined was deliberately induced. Both unsolved."

Meera's grip tightened on the phone. "Someone is systematically collecting Ashokan fragments."

"Not just collecting. Cataloguing. The London theft — the thieves took only the Indian manuscripts and left everything else. Jewelry, paintings, watches on the dresser. They knew exactly what they wanted and exactly where it was. That's not opportunistic theft. That's targeted acquisition based on prior intelligence." His voice sharpened — the clipped precision of an intelligence officer presenting a threat assessment to a superior. "My unit sees a lot of heritage crime, Desai. Most of it is opportunistic — thieves take what's valuable and portable, fence it through established channels, move on. This is different. The selectivity is the signature. They're not stealing for market value. They're stealing for content. They know what the manuscripts say."

"How does Prometheus fit?"

"Directly, I think, but I can't prove it yet. The foundation is well-structured — layered, with legitimate operations providing cover for the collection program. The legitimate side — digitization projects in Syria and Iraq, training programs for Middle Eastern archivists, conference sponsorships — accounts for roughly sixty percent of their annual activity and generates the public profile that attracts grants and positive press. The collection operation appears to be a separate program, run personally by the director, outside the normal organizational structure."

"Who is the director?"

"Dr. Lena Voss. German-American. Forty-one years old. PhD in digital humanities from Humboldt University, Berlin. Dissertation on open access to endangered manuscript collections." Meera heard the click of keystrokes — Rathore reading from a dossier, the product of hours of intelligence work compressed into a voice on a phone line. "She founded Prometheus in 2016 with a combination of personal funds and institutional grants. Prior to that, she worked for the Open Knowledge Foundation in Cambridge, UK, and did a postdoctoral fellowship at the Bodleian Library in Oxford, where she had access to the Bodleian's Indian manuscript collection — one of the largest in Europe."

"Academic credentials."

"Impressive ones. She's published extensively — fifteen peer-reviewed papers on open access, digital preservation, and the ethics of knowledge gatekeeping. Her work is cited in the field. She's respected by her peers. She speaks at conferences. She sits on UNESCO advisory panels."

"The perfect cover."

"Or genuine conviction. The two aren't mutually exclusive. People who believe they're right are often the most dangerous." Rathore paused. She heard the click of a keyboard — he was reading from his notes. "There's something else. I had RAW pull her travel records — visa applications, airline manifests, hotel bookings where available." She heard the satisfaction in his voice — the tone of an intelligence officer who's found the thread and knows it leads somewhere. This was what Rathore lived for: the moment when disparate data points resolved into a pattern, when the noise dropped away and the signal emerged, sharp and undeniable. "In the last three years, she's visited Japan twice, Turkey three times, the UAE four times, the United Kingdom six times, Portugal twice, Uzbekistan once, and the Vatican twice."

Meera went still. Every one of those locations was connected to the Ashokan network — directly or through the historical scattering of knowledge along trade routes and missionary paths. Japan: Buddhist monasteries that might have received transmitted manuscripts. Turkey: caravanserai on the Silk Road. UAE: Rashid's collection. UK: Rothwell's archive and the British Museum's Indian holdings. Portugal: colonial-era collections from Goa. Uzbekistan: Central Asian Silk Road sites. The Vatican: the Apostolic Archive's Jesuit missions to India and Japan.

"She's mapping it," Meera said. "She's tracing the scattering. Following the same routes the Buddhist monks took two thousand years ago."

"She's been ahead of us the entire time. We were guarding the repositories. She was collecting the fragments."

The implications settled over Meera like cold water. The Padmanabhaswamy repository was secure — sealed beneath a temple, protected by mechanisms that only a handful of people could operate. The Newport site was destroyed — burned in the fire that had nearly killed them both. But the guardianship had always assumed the danger came from people trying to break into the vaults. It had never occurred to anyone — not her father, not Kesavan, not the committee — that the knowledge might have leaked centuries ago, scattered across continents by Buddhist monks and Silk Road traders, and that someone with enough patience and resources might reassemble it piece by piece from the scattered fragments.

"The guardianship," she said quietly. "We've been guarding the wrong perimeter."

"I'll send you the full file on Voss and Prometheus. Secure channel."

"Rathore — where is she now?"

"According to her last known movements? Berlin. But her travel patterns suggest she moves every few days. She's active. She's systematic. And she has a head start that we may not be able to close."

"Keep digging. I want to know everything about her. Family, funding, contacts, servers. If she's collecting digital scans of manuscripts from a dozen countries, she's storing them somewhere — a data center, cloud infrastructure, something physical and traceable."

"I'm already on it."

He hung up. Meera sat in the quiet of the loft, the Voss file glowing on her laptop screen, and thought about a woman in Berlin who had spent a decade tracing the same network Meera had spent two years guarding. One of them was protecting the knowledge. One of them was hunting it. And in the space between protection and acquisition, two people were dead.

She was no longer sure whose side she was on. She was only sure of one thing: Voss had a ten-year head start, and the dead were still counting.

---

## Chapter 8

The Prometheus Foundation occupied a converted warehouse on the edge of Kreuzberg, three blocks from the Spree. Meera found the address through corporate registry records — Berlin maintained meticulous public databases, a legacy of postwar transparency laws written by a generation that had learned, in the most terrible way possible, what secrecy cost.

She'd spent the afternoon reading everything she could find. The website was polished. Professional. The kind of design that said "we have money and we want you to know it, but tastefully." Dark backgrounds, high-resolution photography of manuscripts being carefully handled by gloved hands, pull quotes from satisfied partner institutions. The typography was immaculate — a clean sans-serif for body text, a classical serif for headings, the visual language of institutional credibility. Each project page featured professional photography: archivists in white gloves, manuscripts on cradles under LED panels, the warm glow of ancient parchment against the cool blue of digital monitors. The images told a story of competence, care, and moral purpose.

The mission statement, rendered in elegant sans-serif type: *Liberating suppressed heritage for all humanity.* Projects listed: digitizing endangered archives in Syria, Iraq, and Yemen. Training Iraqi archivists in digital preservation techniques. Funding open-access publication of archaeological reports from excavations in Afghanistan and Uzbekistan. Partnering with the University of Tokyo on a Buddhist manuscript imaging project. Each project had its own page, its own progress bar, its own testimonials from partner institutions. The website had clearly been built by professionals — the kind of professionals who charged six figures for a corporate identity package and delivered something that made you trust the organization before you'd read a single word.

All legitimate. All worthwhile. All the kind of work that any heritage professional would applaud and any government agency would approve. The perfect operation — hiding in plain sight, doing good work while pursuing a parallel agenda that none of the partner institutions suspected.

Meera read the staff bios. Fourteen employees. A mix of digital archivists, preservation specialists, software developers, and project managers. European and Middle Eastern backgrounds. PhDs from respected institutions — Leiden, SOAS, Humboldt, Chicago. None of them flagged in any law enforcement database. None of them had connections to intelligence services, private military contractors, or known antiquities smuggling networks.

Then she found the funding page. Prometheus was supported by a mix of European Union cultural grants, private donations, and what the website described as "strategic partnerships with like-minded organizations dedicated to preserving human knowledge." The grant numbers were public — Berlin required transparency from registered nonprofits. Prometheus received approximately €4.2 million per year.

The grants accounted for about €1.8 million. The private donations made up the remaining €2.4 million. The donor list was anonymous — German law permitted it, a provision originally designed to protect political donors that had been co-opted by organizations that preferred not to disclose their funding sources.

Meera clicked through the financial disclosures with the methodical focus of someone who'd spent years tracing money trails for the FBI's Art Crime Team. The EU grants were legitimate — she cross-referenced them against the EU's public database and found matching entries for cultural preservation initiatives in the Mediterranean and Middle East. The private donations were the black box. €2.4 million annually, funneled through what the website called "a donor-advised fund managed by a Berlin-based financial institution." That meant a layer of institutional insulation between Prometheus and whoever was actually paying the bills. Meera made a note: find the financial institution. Follow the money.

Claire leaned over her shoulder, reading the screen. They were at the dining table that served as their joint workstation — two laptops, a shared monitor, a bowl of almonds neither of them had touched. The afternoon light was fading, the industrial windows casting long shadows across the brick walls. The loft had that particular late-afternoon quality — the light turning from white to gold to amber, the shadows lengthening across the hardwood floor, the sense of a day winding down while their work was just beginning.

"Four million euros," Claire said. "That's not a nonprofit budget. That's an intelligence budget." She pulled a highlighter from behind her ear — a habit from her Army days, always a writing implement within reach — and marked a line on the printout in front of her. "An NGO running cultural preservation in conflict zones needs maybe one-point-five to operate legitimately. Two million at the high end. Four-point-two means they're funding something else entirely."

"Or someone else," Meera said.

"It's both. The legitimate work pays for the infrastructure. The offices, the servers, the staff salaries, the travel budgets, the conference sponsorships. All tax-deductible, all publicly documented, all generating the kind of institutional credibility that opens doors. The collection operation runs underneath, invisible to anyone who isn't looking for it."

"What are they collecting?"

Meera pulled up the INTERPOL reports Rathore had forwarded — PDF files with the official headers of seven national law enforcement agencies, cross-referenced by the Cultural Heritage division. She'd printed them earlier, spreading the pages across the table in a grid — incident reports, evidence logs, forensic summaries, witness statements — the documentary debris of crimes that no one had yet connected except Rathore's antiquities unit. "Indian manuscripts with Brahmi inscriptions. Every theft in the last six months targeted materials connected to the Ashokan knowledge network. London: Mughal-era court records referencing a 'hidden archive in the eastern hills.' Berlin: a copper plate with Brahmi numerals describing what appears to be a mathematical formula. Dubai: Rashid's entire collection of forty-seven Indian manuscripts, including three palm-leaf bundles that were catalogued as 'origin uncertain — possibly Gandharan.'"

She tapped the London report with her fingertip. "Look at this. The Mayfair burglary — the thieves entered through a rear window that faced a service alley. No street cameras, no witnesses. The alarm system was a commercial unit, standard for luxury residences. They bypassed it in under ninety seconds. That's not amateur hour. That's someone who had the floor plan, the security codes, and a practiced team."

"All Brahmi?"

"All Ashokan-era or derived from the Ashokan tradition. Someone at Prometheus recognized the pattern — they know what these fragments are, not just their market value but their content. They're not stealing artifacts. They're stealing knowledge."

Claire sat back. Her Army training showed in how she processed information — linear, threat-focused, action-oriented. She didn't ask why. She asked what next. She picked up one of the printouts and turned it over, studying the back as if the blank page might contain the answer to a question she hadn't yet articulated.

"Do we go to Berlin?"

"Not yet. I need more. Right now, Prometheus is a person of interest, not a suspect. If I show up asking questions, Voss will know we're onto her. She'll lock down whatever she's built — move the servers, shred the documents, disappear the manuscripts. We'd lose any chance of understanding what she's assembled."

"So what do we need?"

"The third thread." Meera pulled up her email. Priya had sent a message at 3 AM New York time — midnight in Pune — with the subject line: *You need to see this immediately.* The timestamp told Meera everything about Priya's state of mind: she'd worked past midnight, which meant the discovery had bypassed her normal caution, and she'd used the word "immediately," which she never did. Priya's emails were measured, qualified, hedged with academic caveats. "Immediately" meant she'd found something that stripped away her professional hedging and left only urgency.

She opened it. A photograph. A page of a manuscript, written in a flowing cursive script that was neither Japanese nor Chinese, with marginal annotations in classical Japanese. At the bottom, in a different hand, a series of mathematical notation that Meera didn't recognize but which Priya clearly had.

Priya's note read: *This was sent to me by Dr. Kenji Murakami, University of Tokyo. He's been studying it for three years. He believes it's a medieval Japanese mathematical text — wasan — from a Zen monastery in Kyoto. He contacted me because of my recent Brahmi translation work. Meera, the mathematical notation in the margins contains Brahmi-derived numerals. And the prime factorization method described in the main text is functionally identical to the algorithm from Padmanabhaswamy.*

Meera read the note twice. The first time for content. The second time for implication. Functionally identical. Not similar. Not related. Identical. The same mathematical insight, preserved in a different language, on a different continent, in a different century — and still recognizable to someone who knew what to look for.

Meera stared at the photograph. The notation. The script. The shape of the numbers in the margins — recognizably Brahmi, but adapted, modified by centuries of copying by scribes who didn't understand the original symbols.

The knowledge had reached Japan.

Claire saw her face. "What?"

"Remember what Ashoka built? The repositories weren't the whole system. He also sent knowledge outward — along trade routes, through Buddhist missionaries, across the Silk Road, across the sea lanes. We always assumed the scattered fragments were lost. Destroyed by time. Forgotten."

"And?"

"They weren't. They were preserved. In monasteries. In private collections. In archives. In cedar chests in the back of Zen temple storehouses in the hills of Kyoto. For two thousand years, pieces of the Ashokan knowledge have been sitting in libraries from Japan to Portugal, waiting for someone to recognize them for what they are."

"Someone like Voss."

"Someone like Voss." Meera said it a second time because the repetition made it real — the way saying a diagnosis aloud confirms what the test results have already revealed. Voss wasn't an abstract threat. She was a specific woman with a specific plan, moving through the world with purpose and resources, and she was ahead of them. Far ahead. The breadth of the scattering meant the number of fragments was potentially enormous, and Voss had been collecting them for a decade while the guardianship watched two locked doors.

Meera closed her eyes. The dread sat heavier than anything she'd felt since Newport — heavier, perhaps, because at Newport the threat had been direct and physical: a fire, a collapsing building, a man with a gun. This was worse. This was the realization that everything she'd believed about the guardianship — everything her father had believed, everything the committee had been built on — was founded on an incomplete understanding of the system they were protecting.

She'd been guarding two vaults while the knowledge had been scattered across a dozen countries, preserved by monks and librarians and collectors who had no idea what they were keeping.

"We need to call Rathore again," she said. "And then we need to call Tokyo."

---

## Chapter 9

The call connected at 11:47 PM New York time — 12:47 PM in Tokyo. Dr. Kenji Murakami answered on the second ring, which told Meera two things: he'd been expecting the call, and he'd been sitting at his phone waiting for it.

He had the gentle voice of a man who spent his life in libraries. He spoke English carefully, weighing each word before he released it, the habit of someone for whom imprecision was a professional failing.

"Dr. Sharma told me you might call." He sounded pleased — relieved, even. "I have been hoping someone would take an interest in this manuscript for a very long time."

Meera was in the loft's spare bedroom, which served as a private call room when she needed to discuss guardianship matters out of Claire's earshot — though these days, Claire heard most of it anyway. The small desk was cluttered with printouts: Priya's Brahmi numeral charts, Rathore's INTERPOL bulletins, a satellite photo of the Prometheus Foundation's Kreuzberg office she'd pulled from a real estate database. Through the thin wall, she could hear Claire on the extension, listening.

The September rain had started again — the third night in a row. It tapped against the loft's industrial windows like fingers drumming a patient rhythm, and the radiator in the corner clicked and groaned, fighting a losing battle against the draft. Meera pulled her cardigan tighter.

"Dr. Murakami, can you tell me how you found the manuscript?"

"Three years ago, I was cataloguing the mathematical holdings of Buddhist monasteries in the Kyoto region. A hobby — my official work is on Edo-period wasan, the traditional Japanese mathematical tradition. But I have always been interested in the transmission of mathematical knowledge from the Asian mainland to Japan." A pause — she heard the creak of a chair, the faint sounds of an academic office. "The monastery in question is a Rinzai Zen temple in the eastern hills of Kyoto. I will not name it without their permission — they value their privacy. The abbot allowed me to examine their library, and in a cedar chest in the back of the sutra storehouse, I found this manuscript."

He described it with a scholar's precision — the kind of precision that came from having examined something so thoroughly that its physical details had become a kind of intimacy. Hand-copied on mulberry-bark paper, bound in the traditional *watoji* style, four holes along the spine edge with braided cotton cords threading the signatures. The paper was *washi* — the high-quality kind used for sutra copying, thick and fibrous, with a faint sheen that told Murakami it had been treated with *gofun*, a white pigment made from crushed oyster shells. The manuscript was perhaps forty pages. It had survived in remarkable condition, protected from light and moisture by the cedar chest's natural insecticidal properties.

The main text was in classical Japanese with Chinese characters — *kanbun* — describing a method for analyzing the properties of large numbers. The marginal annotations were in a script Murakami hadn't recognized — until he'd seen Priya's recent papers on Brahmi numerals.

"The marginalia are Brahmi-derived," he said. "I am certain of this. The numerals are a variant — Japanese scribes adapted the shapes over centuries of copying — but the underlying system is Indian. Brahmi. The same script you find on the Ashoka Pillar at Sarnath."

Meera's hand found the copper disc beneath her shirt. She pressed her thumb against its edge — a small, sharp pressure that focused her thoughts. "And the mathematical content?"

"This is the remarkable part. The main text describes a method for decomposing large numbers into their constituent primes. It is systematic. It is general — not limited to specific cases but applicable to any integer. And it is completely unlike any factorization method in the European tradition."

"Could you explain the method to me?"

"Over the phone? It would be difficult. The notation is — unique. A hybrid of Japanese mathematical conventions and what I now understand to be Brahmi numeral placement. But the core insight is elegant. The author understood that every composite number has a unique prime signature — a fingerprint, if you will. The method works by reconstructing that signature through a series of modular reductions. It is not fast by modern computational standards — it would not compete with a computer — but it is mathematically complete. A human being could, given enough time, factor any integer using only this method and paper."

Meera's throat tightened. A human being could factor any integer. The RSA algorithm's security depended on the computational difficulty of exactly that task. The method didn't need to be fast. It needed to be correct — and if the insight was correct, a computer scientist could optimize it. Accelerate it. Turn a medieval meditation into a modern weapon.

She forced her voice to stay level. "Dr. Murakami, I need to ask you something, and I need you to be honest. Have you shown this manuscript to anyone else?"

A pause. Not evasive — thoughtful. She heard him shift in his chair. "I have discussed it with colleagues in the wasan community. Three colleagues, all at Japanese universities. None of them recognized the Brahmi numerals. And there was one other person. A woman from a European foundation who visited the monastery last month. She was very interested in the manuscript. She offered to fund its digitization."

Meera's pulse quickened — a sudden acceleration she felt in her wrists, her temples, the base of her throat. "What was her name?"

"Dr. Lena Voss. She was quite knowledgeable about Indian mathematical traditions. Unusually so. She recognized the Brahmi numerals in the margins immediately — within minutes of opening the manuscript. She told me the manuscript was evidence of a 'knowledge transmission network' connecting ancient India to Japan through Buddhist monks. I was impressed. Most Western scholars have no idea this connection existed."

Meera gripped the edge of the desk. Voss hadn't stumbled onto the manuscript. She'd gone looking for it.

"How long was she there?"

"Two days. She examined the manuscript thoroughly. She photographed every page with the abbot's permission — high-resolution images, using a portable cradle and LED panels she'd brought with her. Professional equipment. She was very respectful — she brought gifts for the monastery, she attended morning meditation, she sat with the monks during tea ceremony. She was genuinely interested in the history. The abbot was quite taken with her."

"Did she say where she was going next?"

"She mentioned Istanbul. Something about Silk Road archaeological work. She said she was tracing the 'knowledge routes' of Central Asia — a phrase I found striking. She spoke about it with great passion."

Meera closed her eyes. Tokyo. Istanbul. Dubai. Zurich. London. Berlin. Voss wasn't visiting random archives. She was tracing the entire Ashokan scattering — the knowledge network that had distributed fragments across the ancient world. And she'd been doing it for years, moving from site to site with the patient, systematic thoroughness of a scholar building a bibliography. Except the bibliography she was building could compromise global digital security.

"Dr. Murakami, I need to ask you not to discuss this manuscript with anyone else. Not colleagues, not students, not other researchers. Can you do that?"

"Dr. Desai, your tone suggests this is more than an academic matter."

"It is. I can't explain how I know, but the mathematical content of that manuscript — the factorization method — has implications for modern security systems. If it became public before the right people had time to prepare, the consequences could be severe."

Silence. Through the wall, Meera heard Claire end a call and start another — the rapid-fire efficiency of someone working a contact list. The rain intensified, drumming harder against the glass.

Then Murakami spoke again, and his voice had changed. The scholarly warmth was still there, but underneath it was something harder — the voice of a man who had lived long enough to recognize a moral problem that couldn't be solved with more research.

"I am a scholar, Dr. Desai. I have spent my life pursuing knowledge. The idea of suppressing a discovery is deeply uncomfortable for me. My father was a professor of mathematics at Kyoto University. He believed — as I believe — that knowledge belongs to everyone."

"I'm not asking you to suppress it. I'm asking you to give us time. A few weeks. Maybe less."

"The monastery has preserved this manuscript for perhaps eight hundred years. A few more weeks is not much to ask." Another pause, longer this time. "But I must ask — how did a medieval Japanese mathematical text become a matter of international security?"

Meera considered her answer carefully. She owed him truth, but not all of it. "Because the knowledge in that manuscript is two thousand years old. It was created by people who understood mathematics at a level we're only now rediscovering. It's been waiting for someone to find it. You're not the first person to find it, Dr. Murakami. But you might be the only one who found it without understanding what it could do."

"I see." His voice was quiet. He did see — or at least he saw enough. "Very well. I will not share the manuscript until you tell me it is safe to do so. I will also contact my three colleagues and ask them to refrain from discussing it. I cannot guarantee their compliance, but I will try."

"Thank you. One more thing. The photographs Dr. Voss took — do you know if she shared them with anyone?"

"She did not say. But she photographed every page. Every page, Dr. Desai. Including the marginalia."

Every page. High-resolution. Professional equipment. Voss now had a complete digital copy of a manuscript containing a prime factorization method that predated the RSA algorithm by two millennia.

Meera thanked him and ended the call. The loft was quiet except for the rain and the radiator's mechanical breathing. Through the wall, she could hear Claire's voice — lower now, deliberate, the tone she used when she was extracting information from a reluctant source.

Meera stared at the photograph of the Kyoto manuscript on her laptop screen. Priya had sent it as a high-resolution attachment — a single page, the mulberry-bark paper yellowed to the color of old ivory, the *kanbun* text flowing in neat vertical columns, the Brahmi-derived numerals in the margins small and precise, like a secret written in a language only the margins could speak.

The knowledge had reached Japan. A thousand years ago — maybe more — Buddhist monks had carried it across the Silk Road, through the mountain passes of Central Asia, along the Yellow River valley, across the Korea Strait, and into the hills above Kyoto, where scribes who could not read Brahmi had copied the numerals stroke by stroke onto mulberry-bark paper, preserving shapes whose meaning they didn't understand but whose sacredness they respected. And there it had sat, in a cedar chest, in the back of a sutra storehouse, in a Zen monastery wrapped in incense smoke and morning meditation, for eight hundred years.

And Lena Voss had been there. Last month. She'd photographed every page. She'd recognized it for what it was. She'd attended tea ceremony with the abbot and brought gifts for the monks and left with the most dangerous mathematical formula of the twenty-first century captured on a portable cradle camera.

Meera was chasing a woman who was not just years ahead of her, but smarter about where to look.

Her phone buzzed. An email from Murakami, timed eleven minutes after their call — he'd moved quickly. One of his three colleagues, a mathematician at Osaka University, had been contacted earlier that week by a European research foundation offering to fund a "collaborative study" of the Kyoto manuscript. The approach was cordial, professional, generous — full access to imaging equipment, travel grants, co-authorship on any resulting publications. The same playbook. Murakami had asked his colleague to forward the foundation's letterhead. The name at the top was one Meera recognized from Claire's printouts on the dining table. *Prometheus Foundation*. Voss had been to Japan, and now Prometheus was working the secondary channel — the academic channel — trying to get a second pair of eyes on the manuscript through the very colleagues Murakami had just promised to silence. Meera forwarded the email to Priya with a single line: *Add this to the pattern. She's circling.*

---

## Chapter 10

In Berlin, it was 6 AM when the alert brought Lena Voss out of the four hours of sleep she allowed herself, and Klaus Richter already dressed and waiting in the kitchen with the kettle on, as if he had been awake for the whole night and most of the one before it.

The alert was a flag from Murakami's university email — a keyword trace Richter had planted eighteen months earlier, when Prometheus first made contact with the wasan community. The trace had never fired. Now it had: Murakami had sent a long message to an address in Pune, and then, eleven minutes later, a forwarded letterhead. *Prometheus Foundation.* The second message had been cc'd to the same Pune address with a single line Richter could read even through the encryption layer the Pune side was using, because Murakami had not encrypted his own outgoing mail: *Add this to the pattern. She's circling.*

*She.*

Voss read the line on the kitchen counter, the kettle clicking off behind her, the grey Kreuzberg dawn filling the window. The Pune address belonged, almost certainly, to Priya Sharma — the Brahmi scholar whose papers had made the Kyoto numerals legible in the first place. And Priya Sharma had one employer who mattered: Meera Desai.

The guardian had found them faster than Voss had planned for. She had hoped for another week of clean collection before the eastern chain noticed the pattern. She had gotten three days.

"How much does she have?" Richter asked. He was standing by the window, his back to her, watching the street the way he always watched streets — reading them for the shape of a threat that had not yet arrived. His laptop bag was already packed. He had not asked whether they were moving. He had assumed they were.

"Enough to know we exist. Enough to know we've been to Kyoto, Dubai, Zurich. Not enough to know what we're building." Voss set the phone down. "The question is whether she's found the cylinder."

"The London cylinder."

"Rothwell's. If Desai is as good as her file suggests, she'll go to Rothwell. Rothwell will give her the Peshawar cylinder — he's been waiting thirty years for someone to ask. And the cylinder points to Sarnath."

Richter turned. "Then we go to Sarnath first."

The kettle had gone quiet. The only sound was the building's ventilation and the distant clatter of an early tram on Oranienstraße. Voss looked at him — at the man she had built this with, the man whose mother's library had burned in a Leipzig basement, the man who had slept four hours in the last forty-eight because the dead-man switches were not yet complete — and felt the loneliness of a leader who could not share the whole of her plan with the people executing it.

"No," she said. "We don't go to Sarnath first. If we show up at the same time as the guardian, we lose the thing we've spent ten years protecting — the advantage of being invisible. Desai doesn't know what she's looking for yet. She has a cylinder and a hypothesis. She does not have the Chronicle. The Chronicle is what matters."

"Then let me get the Sarnath material before she does."

"You'll walk into an Indian heritage site under ASI jurisdiction and excavate beneath the Ashoka Pillar?" Voss almost smiled. "Klaus. We are not the Stasi. We don't send men with guns to archives. We are faster than them because we are quieter than them." She picked up the phone again, scrolled to the relay map she kept on a separate, air-gapped device. "I want the Istanbul tablets secured tonight. The Turkish dig is the one site where physical material still sits uncollected — Brahmi on stone, in the open, at a university excavation that closes for the weekend. Two of our people are already in the country. Send them in. Photograph everything. If the academic channel is closing behind us — and it is, now that Murakami has talked — then we take what's still open before it shuts."

"And Sarnath?"

"Sarnath is a matter of days, not hours. Desai will need ASI clearance, a team, time. We have time. What we don't have is the full metallurgical corpus, and Istanbul closes on Friday." She looked at him. "Go to Istanbul. Run it yourself. Then meet me in Reykjavik on Sunday. The Liberation window holds. Eight days."

Richter nodded. He did not argue. He never argued when the operational logic was sound; he argued only when he believed a principle was being traded for convenience, and he had not yet learned to see this moment as anything other than operational. He would learn. Voss hoped, for both their sakes, that he learned slowly.

He shouldered his bag. At the door he paused. "The guardian. Desai. You said you wanted her to find us."

"I did."

"She's found us faster than you wanted."

"Yes." Voss touched the silver bracelet at her wrist — the old habit, the warmth of the metal against the inside of her thumb. "That's because she's good, Klaus. That's why I want to talk to her instead of around her. The guardians who came before her hid behind doors. This one opens them. I'd rather she opened mine than kicked it down."

He left. The apartment settled into the silence of a space that had just lost its second occupant — a change in the air pressure, in the quality of the quiet. Voss stood at the kitchen counter and looked at the alert one more time. *She's circling.*

Good. Let her circle. Let her follow the road Voss had already walked, and find, at every door she reached, the quiet evidence that someone had been there first — and one piece, left behind on purpose, so the guardian would know exactly where to find her when she was ready.

She picked up her phone and composed a message to an unlisted number in Pune. Then she deleted it without sending. Not yet. The guardian was still circling. When she landed, Voss would be the one to tell her where.

Eight days. The equinox. The wheel was already turning. It only needed one more push.

---

## Chapter 11

They assembled around the dining table at 10 PM: Meera, Claire, and Priya on a video call from Pune. Three time zones, three cities, three women connected by an encrypted video link that Meera had set up after Newport — a small privacy she'd learned to value in the years since leaving the Bureau, where every call was logged and every log was someone else's business.

Priya's face was lit by the blue glow of her laptop; behind her, the spines of bound journals filled a bookshelf from floor to ceiling. She was in her office at Deccan College, working late, as usual. The overhead fluorescents were off — she preferred the desk lamp, a brass angle-poised thing that looked like it had been rescued from a colonial administrator's estate sale. It cast warm shadows across her face and turned the edges of her glasses to copper. The time in Pune was 7:30 AM, but Priya had clearly been at her desk since before dawn.

Meera's laptop sat on a stack of art history monographs — provenance reference guides she used for consulting work. Claire's was on the other side of the table, angled so both of them could see Priya's face. The table between them held the detritus of a long day: two coffee mugs gone cold, a plate of almonds neither of them had touched, printouts of the Prometheus Foundation's website with Claire's handwritten annotations in the margins. Claire wrote in block capitals — Army habit, Meera knew, from years of drafting intelligence reports that had to be legible under field conditions.

"Let me make sure I have this right," Priya said. She had a notebook open — the same thick spiral-bound one she'd been filling since Padmanabhaswamy, its cover now soft with handling, the spiral binding warped where she'd pressed too hard writing. "The wootz steel formula appears in a preprint from Zurich. The researcher is dead. A Dubai collector who owned Indian manuscripts with Brahmi inscriptions is also dead. And a Japanese mathematical manuscript contains a prime factorization method identical to the Padmanabhaswamy algorithm."

"Functionally identical," Meera said. "Different notation, different mathematical language. Same result."

"Three independent surfacings. Three countries. Three types of knowledge — metallurgical, undetermined, mathematical. All derived from the Ashokan knowledge base. All appearing within months of each other."

"That's not coincidence."

"No. It's convergence." Meera heard herself using the word and felt the weight of it — the way it collapsed centuries into a single moment. "Modern technology is catching up to ancient knowledge. Materials science, computational linguistics, digital manuscript analysis — the tools exist now to accidentally recreate things that were once locked in repositories. A researcher in Zurich uses an antique crucible and accidentally grows wootz nanotubes. A Japanese mathematician recognizes Brahmi-derived numerals because Priya's papers made them visible. The knowledge is surfacing on its own."

Claire leaned forward. Her forearms rested on the table, hands flat — the posture she fell into when her operational mind was engaging, the same stance Meera had seen in the basement at Newport when Claire was processing a threat assessment. "And Prometheus is collecting the pieces before anyone else notices."

Priya's eyes widened. She pushed her glasses up her nose. "If Voss has been doing this for a decade — visiting monasteries, archives, private collections — she could have assembled a significant portion of the Ashokan knowledge base without ever touching the repositories."

"Which means," Meera said, "the guardianship has a blind spot we never considered. We've been guarding the vaults. The knowledge wasn't in the vaults. It was scattered across the entire ancient world."

The words hung in the air. Meera felt them land — not just on Priya and Claire, but on herself. Eighteen months as guardian, and she'd been watching the wrong doors.

"Through Buddhist missionaries," Priya said. Her voice quickened — this was her ground. She was on her feet now, out of frame, voice coming from the left. "Same roads as the monks — that's the key. Wherever the dharma went, the math went with it. Sri Lanka, Central Asia, the Silk Road, the sea routes. It rode along on the Buddhism, basically."

"How far did it go?" Claire asked.

Priya's face reappeared in the frame — she'd pulled a book from the shelf and was holding it open, one finger marking a page. "To Japan." She read from the book without looking at it — she'd memorized the passage long ago. "The monk Kobo Daishi — Kukai — traveled to China in 804 CE. He studied Sanskrit with a monk who'd been educated at Nalanda. He returned to Japan in 806 carrying Sanskrit texts. He founded a monastery on Mount Koya that still exists." She set the book down and looked directly into the camera. "The transmission path is documented: Indian mathematical knowledge → Buddhist monks → China → Korea → Japan → Zen monasteries in Kyoto. The knowledge traveled the same way Buddhism traveled — along the routes of faith."

"So somewhere in that transmission," Claire said, "the prime factorization method was copied, translated, and preserved in a Zen monastery for twelve hundred years."

"Exactly. And not just Japan." Priya was pacing again, the edge of her kurta crossing the frame. "The numerals we call 'Arabic'? Indian. They went out the same roads — to Persia, to Baghdad, to Europe. Al-Khwarizmi credited his Indian sources by name. The knowledge didn't scatter in one act. It percolated — carried along the trade routes the way dye spreads through water."

Meera looked at the notes she'd spread across the table — Zurich to Dubai to Tokyo to Berlin to London, each arrow dated and sourced. "So the vaults were never the whole system. Ashoka hid the concentrated stores — but he scattered fragments along the same roads his monks walked. The stores stayed sealed. The fragments survived in monasteries and private collections across Eurasia, unread, for two millennia — the math disguised as scripture, the medicine as monastic recipes, the script itself a dead language nobody could parse."

"Until now," Claire said. "Now we can read Brahmi. Now we have materials science that can identify ancient metallurgical processes. Now we have computational tools that can analyze mathematical structures. The fragments are becoming legible."

"And Prometheus is collecting them," Meera said. "Systematically. Globally. They've been at it for years. They've visited Japan, Turkey, the UAE, the UK, Portugal, Uzbekistan, the Vatican. Every location is connected to the Ashokan scattering network."

Priya sat down. The chair creaked. Her face was serious now, the academic excitement replaced by something harder — the look she got when the data stopped being interesting and started being dangerous. "Meera, if Voss has collected enough fragments, she could reconstruct the core knowledge base. The mathematics, the metallurgy, the medicine — all of it. Without ever breaking into Padmanabhaswamy or Newport."

"That's what I'm afraid of."

"What's the play?" Claire said. She'd been quiet through the history; now she leaned in, forearms on the table.

"Two tracks," Meera said. "First, map the scattering — every fragment, every location, every transmission path. If we know where the pieces are, we know where Voss goes next. Priya, can you build that map?"

"I can start now. Buddhist missionary routes, Silk Road networks, Nalanda's international reach, the transmission of mathematics and medicine into Persia, China, Japan. Give me forty-eight hours."

"Second track: what does Voss plan to do with the collection? Private archive? A buyer? Something else?"

Claire picked up the Prometheus printout — the dark homepage, the careful type — and dropped it back on the table. "Mission statement. 'Liberating suppressed heritage.'" She looked at Meera. "What if it isn't spin. What if she means release it. All of it. At once."

The room went cold. The rain had stopped, and in the sudden silence Meera could hear the old building settling — a creak from the floorboards, the distant hum of the elevator shaft. RSA. The nerve-regeneration formula. The wootz process. All of it, on the open internet, the same hour. No gatekeepers. No preparation. No time for the world to absorb it.

"That's catastrophic," she said. "The cryptography alone—"

"It starts with the cryptography and spreads from there," Claire said. Her voice had gone flat — the way it did when she was working through a worst case. "Banking first. Then government communications, then military encryption — all of it, the same week. It would make 2008 look minor. And the intelligence agencies — the Americans, the Chinese, the Russians — none of them sit quiet while their ciphers break. That's not a market event, Meera. That's a security crisis."

"And the medicine," Priya said. She'd stopped pacing; she sat very still now, hands flat on the desk. "Released with no trials, no protocols, no studies. The pharma companies patent the derivatives inside a month. The people who need the cures can't afford them. That's the best case. The worst — ancient medicine wasn't gentle. Some of those formulas would kill you before they cured you."

"We need to get ahead of her," Meera said. "Not follow her trail. Find out what she has, what she's planning, and how we stop it."

"One problem," Claire said. "We're not FBI anymore. We have no jurisdiction, no authority, no institutional backing. We're two women in a Tribeca loft with a consulting business and a very expensive secret."

Meera looked at her. Claire met her gaze steadily — the steadiness of a woman who'd chosen this burden and refused to put it down.

"I'll call Rathore," Meera said. "He has resources. And I have an idea about how to get inside Prometheus."

Claire's expression didn't change, but something behind her eyes shifted — the same shift Meera had learned to read in the field, the micro-expression that said: *I'm going to hate this, but I trust you enough to hear it.* "I'm not going to like this idea, am I?"

"Probably not."

---

## Chapter 12

Meera called Rathore at midnight. She expected him to answer — Rathore kept the hours of a man who'd spent his career in the spaces between governments, where crises didn't respect time zones. He picked up on the third ring, and his voice was alert, no trace of sleep. Delhi was nine and a half hours ahead. It was 9:30 AM there. He'd already been at his desk for hours.

She laid it out: the Prometheus Foundation, the funding anomalies, the INTERPOL pattern, Murakami's manuscript, Voss's visit to Kyoto. She spoke for six minutes without interruption. When she finished, Rathore was silent for six seconds — his longest silence in their entire working relationship, and in that silence Meera heard the sound of a man reassembling his understanding of a threat landscape.

"You want to walk into the Prometheus Foundation and ask them to partner with you."

"I want to walk in posing as a heritage consultant looking for digitization partners. It's what Desai Heritage Consulting actually does. It's a legitimate cover. They'll check our references — they'll find real clients, real projects, real receipts. The Boston Museum of Fine Arts, the Metropolitan provenance workshop, the Getty restitution panel. All documented, all verifiable."

"And when you're inside?"

"I observe. I look at their server infrastructure, their security, their physical holdings. I get a sense of their operation — how many people, how much equipment, how organized. And I meet whoever's willing to meet a potential donor."

"Voss won't be there. Directors of international foundations don't meet with small consulting firms."

"Someone will. And that someone might tell me things Voss wouldn't."

Rathore exhaled — the sound of a man calculating risk, running scenarios, weighing outcomes. She'd heard that exhalation before: in Thiruvananthapuram, when she'd told him about the Padmanabhaswamy vault. In Newport, when the first explosion had shaken the foundations. It was the sound of a professional accepting that the situation was worse than he'd assumed.

"It's not a bad plan. But it has a flaw. If Prometheus is connected to the Zurich murder and the Dubai crash, walking into their office is walking into a hostile intelligence operation with no backup."

"That's why I'm asking you for backup."

"Meera — I've been doing this for twenty-two years. I've seen people walk into what they thought was a routine meeting and walk out into an ambulance. Or not walk out at all. Prometheus isn't a nonprofit. It's an intelligence operation dressed in cultural clothing. The staff probably includes at least one person with security training — every operation like this has one. A spotter. Someone whose job is to read visitors, assess threat levels, flag anomalies. You walk in there with your FBI instincts and your guardian's urgency, and a trained spotter will read you in thirty seconds."

"So I don't walk in with urgency. I walk in as a consultant looking for work. Boring, professional, slightly desperate for new clients. That's not a stretch — Desai Heritage Consulting could use the business."

A pause. She could almost hear him smiling — the thin, reluctant smile of a man acknowledging that the argument had landed. "It's still risky."

"Everything's risky. The question is whether the risk is proportional to the intelligence gain."

"And if they make you?"

"Then I'm an American heritage consultant who got lost and wandered into the wrong office. There's no crime in visiting a nonprofit. And if they're as sophisticated as you think, they won't do anything overt — too much attention, too much exposure. They'll smile, give me coffee, and show me the public-facing operation. Which tells me something too."

Another pause — longer. "I'm in Delhi."

"Can you get to Berlin?"

A pause — shorter this time, more practical. She heard the creak of his chair, the faint sound of traffic through his office window. Delhi's morning traffic, the horns and engines that provided the permanent background music of India's capital. "RAW has a cultural attaché at the Indian Embassy. He's one of mine — trained by me, vetted by me, loyal to the institution, not the government of the day. He can provide logistics and emergency extraction if needed. His name is Arjun Bhat. I'll brief him tonight."

"Good. Because I also need to know about the third chain."

"What third chain?"

The words had been forming in Meera's mind since Priya's video call — an idea that had started as a suspicion and hardened into something closer to certainty. "Priya's research. The scattering network. If the knowledge traveled along Buddhist routes through Central Asia, there should be fragments in the Pamir Mountains, in the former Kushan territories, in the spaces between India and the Mediterranean. The Padmanabhaswamy priests were the eastern chain. The Aldridge family was the western chain. But if Ashoka distributed knowledge along the Silk Road, there should be a third guardian tradition — a Central Asian lineage. Voss visited Uzbekistan. She went to the Vatican. She's tracing something we don't know about."

Rathore's voice sharpened. The ambient noise on his end quieted — he'd closed a window, or moved to a more private space. "You think there are guardians we haven't accounted for."

"I think the guardianship was never just two chains. I think it was a network — and we've only been seeing two nodes of it. And I think Voss knows more about it than we do."

Silence. The Delhi traffic hummed faintly, ten thousand miles and a world away from the quiet of the Tribeca loft. Then: "I'll start digging. Central Asia, Kushan Empire, Buddhist manuscript traditions in the Pamirs. If there's a third lineage, RAW's Central Asian intelligence network should find traces. We have assets in Dushanbe, Tashkent, and Almaty — the old Soviet republics where Russian intelligence once operated freely and now we do."

"And the Vatican," Meera added. "Voss went there too. If the Catholic Church has been guarding Ashokan fragments — and two thousand years of monastic manuscript preservation suggests they might have — then the Vatican isn't just a committee member. It's a node in the network."

"The Holy See is a sovereign state. I can't exactly send an operative into the Vatican archives."

"You don't need to. Kurien is on the committee. He has access. If there's something in the Vatican's collections, he'd know about it."

"You want me to investigate a priest."

"I want you to find out whether the Vatican has been sitting on Ashokan fragments for the last two thousand years. The priest is a means, not an end."

Rathore made a sound that wasn't quite a laugh. "You know, Desai, there are days when I think the only thing more dangerous than the knowledge you're guarding is the network of people who know about it."

"Every day is one of those days. That's why we're having this conversation at midnight."

"How long?"

"Three days. Maybe four."

"We don't have three days. The Zurich preprint is gone, but the knowledge is out. Every day, another fragment might surface. Another researcher might accidentally recreate something dangerous. Voss is collecting. We need to be collecting too — information, not artifacts."

"I understand the urgency, Desai. I also understand that rushing into an intelligence operation without adequate preparation gets people killed. I've buried colleagues who moved too fast."

Meera heard the weight in his voice — not a rebuke, but a statement of fact. She didn't need to be reminded. She understood why he was reminding her anyway.

"Three days," she said. "But I'm going to Berlin tomorrow. Claire and I both. Bhat can meet us at BER."

"Tempelhof is closed. BER's the one. I'll have him send you contact details within the hour. Desai — one more thing."

"What?"

"Be careful. If Voss is what you think she is — someone who's been building this operation for a decade — she didn't get this far by being careless. The people at Prometheus will be screened. They'll check you. And if they find something they don't like, they won't call the police."

The line went dead. Meera set the phone on the table and stared at it for a long moment. Through the wall, she could hear Claire's voice — still on the phone, still working her contacts, the steady hum of a woman who had turned vigilance into a second language.

Meera sat still, thinking about her father. He'd died of a disease the knowledge could have cured, guarding a system that was incomplete — a wall built around two vaults while the real treasure had been scattered across the world, hidden in plain sight in cedar chests and museum basements and the margins of Zen manuscripts.

She opened her laptop and began making a list. Everything they knew about Prometheus. Everything they didn't. Every question the Berlin visit needed to answer.

At 1 AM, she reached the last question on the list — the one she had been circling all night without letting herself write it down. She wrote it now, alone, in the quiet loft, with the rain starting again against the windows:

*What do I do if she's right?*

She stared at it. She did not have an answer. And somewhere — she was certain of this now, the way she was certain the rain would stop by morning — Lena Voss was awake too, building something, and knew that the guardian was closing in.

---

## Chapter 13

She told Claire over breakfast the next morning. Toast, black coffee, the September light grey through the industrial windows. The rain had finally stopped sometime in the small hours, leaving the streets slick and the air smelling of wet asphalt — one of New York's rare clean mornings, the kind that made the city feel almost habitable.

Claire listened, chewed, swallowed. She didn't look up from her plate. "Berlin. Today."

"Morning flight. Six hours. We can be at Prometheus by this evening."

"We."

"You don't have to come."

"Meera." Claire set down her toast. Her eyes were level, unamused. "I've been in worse rooms than a Berlin nonprofit. I'm coming."

"It might not be just a visit."

"It's never just a visit with you."

Meera smiled. It was rare enough that Claire noticed — a slight softening around the eyes that she hadn't seen in the months since the Zurich preprint first crossed their screen. "Fair point."

They packed light. Two laptops, encrypted phones, a change of clothes each. Claire added a signal-shielding pouch for the laptops — a Faraday bag, matte black, military-grade, the kind that blocked all electromagnetic signals. A habit from her Army intelligence days that Meera had adopted after Newport, when she'd learned that the things you carried could be read from across a room by anyone with the right scanner. Meera added her FBI credentials — expired, but useful in some situations. The badge opened doors that consulting business cards didn't, even when the badge was no longer active. She also added the small magnifying loupe she'd carried since her art-crime days — a German-made Zeiss lens, ten-power, with a folding brass case worn smooth from years of handling. It sat in the front pocket of her messenger bag, a talisman from a simpler life.

At JFK, waiting at the gate, Meera called Priya one more time. The terminal was crowded with the mid-morning rush — business travelers with rolling bags, families with strollers, a group of teenagers in matching T-shirts on what looked like a school trip. The normalcy of it felt alien, a parallel universe where the most dangerous thing in anyone's day was a delayed flight.

"Tell me about the Japanese manuscript. The factorization method — is it identical to the Padmanabhaswamy algorithm?"

"Not identical. Different mathematical language, different notation, different approach. But the result is the same. Two independent traditions, separated by two thousand years and five thousand miles, both arriving at the same method for decomposing large integers." Priya's voice was tight with the excitement of a scholar who'd found something that broke her models. "The Kyoto manuscript is a scattered fragment — a piece of the Ashokan knowledge that was transmitted, translated, and preserved in a completely different cultural context. Different language, different religious framework, different copying tradition. Same mathematics."

"How is that possible? The algorithm is complex. It wouldn't survive casual copying."

"It wouldn't survive casual copying. But it would survive reverent copying." Priya's voice slowed. "Remember — the monks who preserved this manuscript didn't understand the mathematics. They copied it because it was sacred. The abbot told Murakami that the text contained 'the mind of the Buddha expressed in numbers.' They preserved it as a religious object, not a scientific one. That's why it survived. Faith preserves what curiosity might discard."

She paused, and when she spoke again her voice was quieter — almost reverent, which for Priya was unusual. She was a rationalist by training and temperament, a woman who'd built her career on the assumption that everything could be explained if you looked closely enough. But this was different. "Think about it, Meera. For eight hundred years, monks who had no idea what prime numbers were copied this text onto fresh mulberry-bark paper every few generations — because their predecessors had copied it, and their predecessors before them, back to the monk who first carried it across the Sea of Japan. They preserved it not because they understood it, but because they believed it was holy. The mathematics survived because of faith. Not because of science."

The thought sent a chill through Meera that had nothing to do with the air conditioning.

Meera looked out the terminal window at the grey tarmac. A Lufthansa jet was taxiing toward the runway, its wings catching the thin September light. "Priya — how many more fragments are out there? If the knowledge scattered along every route Ashoka's missionaries traveled, along every branch of the Silk Road, along every maritime trade connection..."

"I don't know. Dozens, maybe. Hundreds. Most are probably lost — destroyed in wars, fires, conquests, the simple decay of organic materials. Paper doesn't last forever. Palm leaves rot. Copper corrodes. But some survived. In monasteries. In caves. In private collections. In museum basements, catalogued as 'Asian manuscripts of uncertain origin' and never studied. The fragments are everywhere, Meera. They've been everywhere for two thousand years. We just couldn't read them until now."

The boarding call came over the speakers — first Lufthansa, then the connecting announcement for Frankfurt and Berlin. Meera stood, shouldered her bag.

"Priya. Build me that map. Every route, every monastery, every known fragment. Everything the historical record tells us about where Ashoka's knowledge traveled."

"I'm already on it. I've pulled the missionary records from the Mahavamsa and the Ashokavadana. I'm cross-referencing with Silk Road archaeological reports from the last fifty years. I've also contacted colleagues at the British Library — their Asian manuscript collection is the largest in Europe, and if there are fragments catalogued under incorrect provenances, they'll find them."

"One more thing." Meera opened the committee portal on her phone — the guardian access she'd carried since Newport, the one that let her see what every member read and when. She'd been meaning to run the access logs since Zurich and hadn't; now, at a departure gate with ten minutes to kill, she did. "Hold on. I'm pulling the metallurgical access logs myself."

She scrolled. The four members with clearance — standard queries, routine patterns, nothing that jumped. She almost closed the app. Then she filtered the raw log instead of the clearance-filtered view, and a fifth entry surfaced that the friendly interface had buried: three months ago, two sessions, four hours total, full read of the wootz sections. The user ID wasn't one of the four.

"Priya. Father Kurien. The Vatican priest. He pulled every page of the metallurgical manuscripts three months ago. Four hours across two sessions."

A pause on the line. "Kurien doesn't have metallurgical clearance."

"I know. The clearance restrictions are advisory — the system doesn't enforce them. He has general committee access, so he could read anything. And he chose to read the wootz sections. All of them."

"Why would he?"

"I don't know yet." Meera's thumb hovered over the timestamp — three months ago, two days before Elena Moretti uploaded her preprint in Zurich. She stared at the date until it blurred. "But the timing isn't random. Kurien was reading the metallurgy two days before a researcher in Zurich accidentally recreated it. I'm not saying he's connected. I'm saying he was looking at the same knowledge, at the same time, from inside the system I'm supposed to be protecting."

"I don't know. But I'm going to ask him."

Meera ended the call and walked toward the gate. Claire fell into step beside her, coat over her arm, expression unreadable. She moved through the terminal the way she moved through every space — aware of exits, sight lines, crowd density. It wasn't paranoia. It was habit, and after three years of living with it, Meera had stopped noticing and started absorbing.

"Problem?" Claire asked.

"Maybe. Kurien read the metallurgical manuscripts three months ago."

"The Vatican priest?"

"The same."

"Is that suspicious?"

"I don't know yet." Meera kept her voice low. The gate was crowded, passengers jostling with bags and boarding passes, the usual pre-boarding chaos. A businessman in a charcoal suit pushed past with his roller bag, phone clamped to his ear, speaking German — a sound that made Meera's ears prick up. Berlin was already in the air around her. "But two people are dead, the knowledge is leaking, and everyone who had access to it is a suspect until I understand what's happening."

"Including committee members?"

"Especially committee members." Meera looked at Priya's notes on her phone — the access log timestamps, Kurien's user ID, the four-hour duration. "Kurien is a Jesuit. Jesuits are scholars — they're trained to pursue knowledge wherever it leads. It's possible he was doing legitimate research. It's also possible he was gathering information for someone else."

"The Vatican has its own archive. Its own secrets."

"The Vatican has the largest collection of uncatalogued manuscripts in the Western world. If Kurien has access to the Ashokan knowledge and the Vatican archive, he's sitting at the intersection of two intelligence networks — and I have no idea what he's doing there."

Claire didn't respond. She handed her boarding pass to the agent and walked down the jetway without looking back.

They boarded the plane. Meera took the window seat, Claire the aisle. The engines spooled up — a deep, resonant vibration that traveled through the airframe and into the armrest. The tarmac slid away. New York shrank beneath them: the grid of streets, the silver thread of the Hudson, the distant smudge of the Statue of Liberty in the harbor haze.

The old dread was back, sitting low in her throat. But beneath it, for the first time, something harder: not certainty — purpose. In eight hours she would walk through the door Lena Voss had built. And Voss, who had spent a decade staying invisible, did not yet know that the woman looking for her had stopped asking whose side she was on.

---

## Chapter 14

Berlin in September was grey stone and sharp air. The city felt cleaned — scoured by history, stripped of pretension, rebuilt with the deliberate plainness of people who'd learned what grandeur cost. Concrete and glass, wide boulevards, the occasional baroque church rising from the grid like a reminder that the city had once had pretensions. Meera had been here once before, during her FBI years, chasing a forged Kandinsky through a Charlottenburg gallery. She remembered the city as efficient and self-serious. It hadn't changed.

What had changed was her. The last time she'd walked these streets, she'd been an FBI agent with a badge and a purpose. Now she was a private consultant with a cover story and a secret that could crash the global economy. The distance she'd traveled — from FBI agent to private consultant to guardian of secrets that could reshape the world — pressed against her like a physical thing.

They landed at BER at 8:40 PM local time. The airport was new, vast, and eerily efficient — the kind of glass-and-steel terminal that made every arrival feel like a transaction. The taxi driver took them through the southern suburbs — wide boulevards, bare trees starting to lose their leaves, cyclists in reflective jackets who seemed to own the roads regardless of hour or weather. Meera watched the city scroll past and thought about surveillance. BER had cameras everywhere — customs, immigration, the taxi rank. If Prometheus was watching for them, they'd already been logged.

They checked into a hotel on Oranienstraße in Kreuzberg. The desk clerk didn't ask questions — a young man with a beard and a German novel face-down on the counter, the kind of person who worked the night shift because it left his days free for more interesting pursuits. Berlin hotels had seen stranger guests than two women arriving at midnight with matching messenger bags and no checked luggage.

Meera didn't sleep well. She lay in the narrow bed, staring at the ceiling — a white expanse broken only by a smoke detector and a water stain that looked like the subcontinent if you squinted — and ran the approach in her mind. Cover story: Desai Heritage Consulting, seeking digitization partners for a collection of South Asian manuscripts belonging to a private American client. The client was real — a Boston collector named Hendricks who'd hired them six months ago to authenticate a set of Nepalese miniatures. The interest was genuine. If Prometheus checked, they'd find a real firm, real clients, real work. The Boston Museum of Fine Arts. The Metropolitan provenance workshop. The Getty restitution panel.

The question was whether Prometheus would see past the cover. These weren't art thieves or smugglers — they were idealists with resources and the discipline of a decade-long operation. The most dangerous combination Meera knew. True believers didn't negotiate. They didn't compromise. And they didn't stop.

At 6 AM, she gave up on sleep and sat at the small desk by the window. Through the glass, Kreuzberg was waking: a bakery across the street opened its shutters, releasing the smell of fresh bread into the cold air; a cyclist in a yellow jacket locked his bike to a railing and disappeared into a cafe; a woman walked a dachshund along the sidewalk, both of them moving with the unhurried certainty of creatures who knew exactly where they were going.

Meera opened her laptop and studied the Prometheus website one more time. The staff page. The project descriptions. The funding reports. She memorized names, titles, project timelines. If Hrubý mentioned a colleague, she wanted to know who they were before he said the name. If he referenced a project, she wanted to have read about it. Information was armor, and she was walking into a building full of people who had more of it than she did.

At 8 AM, she and Claire walked the eleven blocks from Oranienstraße to the Prometheus Foundation headquarters. The morning was cold — 7 degrees, the kind of cold that doesn't bite so much as settle into your bones and wait. Meera wore her leather jacket over a black sweater. Claire wore a grey wool coat that she'd bought at a surplus store in Virginia — Army-issue warmth without the camouflage. They walked at a pace that said *we belong here* — not too fast, not too slow, two professionals on their way to a meeting.

The building was a converted warehouse — four stories of red brick, industrial windows, a facade that had been aggressively modernized with glass and steel. A ground-floor cafe spilled onto the sidewalk, filled with the kind of people who worked in startups and talked about disruption over oat milk lattes. The neighborhood was Kreuzberg at its most Kreuzberg: street art on every wall, vintage clothing stores, a Turkish bakery next to a coworking space, a record shop that appeared to sell vinyl exclusively from the 1970s. The city's famous tolerance for eccentricity was on full display, and Meera noted it the way she noted everything — as context, as background, as the kind of environment where a converted warehouse housing a heritage nonprofit blended in so perfectly it was almost invisible.

The Prometheus entrance was on the side — a glass door with a steel frame, a small bronze plaque: *Prometheus Foundation. Liberating Heritage.* A buzzer. A camera. The camera was high-definition, wide-angle, the kind used in professional security systems. It tracked them as they approached.

Meera pressed the buzzer. A voice, female, young: "Prometheus Foundation. Can I help you?"

"My name is Dr. Meera Desai. I'm with Desai Heritage Consulting in New York. My partner Claire Whitfield and I are visiting Berlin to explore digitization partnerships. We'd like to learn about your work."

A pause. The camera above the door adjusted — a faint mechanical whir, barely audible above the street noise. Someone was looking at them. Meera kept her expression neutral, professional, slightly bored — the face of a consultant who'd had this conversation a hundred times.

"Please wait."

Forty seconds. Meera counted them. Long enough for a security check — an image search, maybe, or a quick scan of the Desai Heritage Consulting website. Long enough for someone to decide whether to let them in.

The door clicked open. A young woman with short purple hair and round glasses met them in the lobby — white walls, polished concrete floor, a reception desk made from a single slab of reclaimed wood. A large photograph behind the desk showed a manuscript being carefully unrolled by gloved hands — the same image from the website, but larger, the texture of the ancient paper visible in high resolution.

"Dr. Desai. I'm Maren, Tomáš Hrubý's assistant. He's our director of digital archives. He can speak with you about our digitization programs." She smiled — genuine, warm, the smile of someone who liked their job. "He's always happy to meet heritage professionals."

They followed Maren up a steel staircase to the second floor. The office was open-plan — workstations, monitors, the quiet hum of a dozen people working in that focused silence that characterized professional spaces where real work got done. The aesthetic was Berlin tech: exposed ductwork, hanging lights, whiteboards covered in project timelines. But among the modern furniture, Meera noticed details that didn't fit. A framed photograph of the Bamiyan Buddhas before their destruction — the massive cliff-face statues that had stood in Afghanistan for fifteen centuries until the Taliban dynamited them in 2001. A case containing what appeared to be a fragment of ancient pottery, glazed in a turquoise pattern that looked Central Asian. A wall map showing conflict zones across the Middle East and Central Asia, with red pins marking active preservation projects. The map had the specificity of an intelligence product — not decorative, but functional.

And at the far end of the floor, behind frosted glass: the server room. Larger than a nonprofit needed. The hum of cooling fans was audible from twenty feet away. The glass was opaque, but the sound told Meera everything she needed to know about the equipment behind it. That wasn't a file server. That was a data center.

Tomáš Hrubý met them at the top of the stairs. He was in his late thirties, Czech, with an earnest face and wire-rimmed glasses that he kept pushing up his nose — a nervous habit that surfaced every few seconds, as if the glasses were perpetually sliding toward an insight he hadn't yet articulated. He wore a corduroy jacket over a T-shirt that read *Information Wants to Be Free* in faded letters. He shook hands with genuine warmth.

"Dr. Desai. Ms. Whitfield. Welcome." His English was fluent, Czech-accented, the consonants slightly harder than a native speaker's. "Your firm's work on the Boston Nepalese collection — I read about it in the ICOM newsletter. Impressive provenance analysis."

Meera was careful. The compliment was genuine, but it was also a signal: *I've already looked you up.* "Thank you. We're small, but we try to be thorough."

"Thoroughness is everything in this field. Come — let me show you what we do."

He turned and led them down the corridor toward the digitization lab, his corduroy jacket brushing the wall as he walked, his T-shirt's faded declaration — *Information Wants to Be Free* — visible between the jacket's lapels. Meera followed, noting the cameras, the sight lines, the location of the emergency exits. Old habits. Useful habits. Behind her, Claire was doing the same thing — two women with law enforcement training walking into a building that looked like a nonprofit and felt like something else.

---

## Chapter 15

Hrubý gave them the tour with the enthusiasm of a man who loved his work and assumed everyone else would love it too. He was a true believer — not in the Ashokan network, which he clearly knew nothing about, but in the principle that knowledge should be preserved and shared. His passion was genuine, his sincerity unmistakable, and Meera found herself liking him despite knowing that the organization he worked for was running a parallel operation he couldn't see.

The first floor housed the digitization lab. Three technicians worked at stations equipped with high-resolution cameras, cradle scanners for fragile manuscripts, and color-calibrated monitors that rendered ancient text with surgical precision. The lab was climate-controlled — 20 degrees, 45 percent humidity — and the technicians wore white cotton gloves as they handled materials, moving with the careful deliberation of people who understood that a single careless breath could destroy a thousand-year-old page.

A Syrian medieval text lay open under a camera — pages of Arabic script on leather-thin parchment, the ink faded to brown, the margins annotated in a different hand. A project Hrubý described with quiet intensity.

"This is from the Zirid library in Aleppo. Survived the civil war by chance — a schoolteacher named Basheer carried it out in a suitcase during the evacuation of eastern Aleppo in 2016. He walked fourteen kilometers through active combat zones with two hundred pages of medieval scholarship strapped to his chest. When he reached safety, he contacted us through the UNESCO emergency heritage network. We flew a team to Turkey to receive it." Hrubý's voice caught slightly. "Basheer died last year. A bombing. But the manuscript survived. We're digitizing it for the Syrian National Archives, who are operating in exile. When the war ends and the archives are rebuilt, the digital copy will be there. That's what we do. We make sure knowledge survives the people who want to destroy it."

Meera studied the manuscript under its glass cradle. The Arabic script was beautiful — fluid, regular, the work of a trained calligrapher. The marginal annotations were denser, more hurried — a scholar working through the text, arguing with it, adding corrections and references. The manuscript had survived war, evacuation, and a fourteen-kilometer walk through a combat zone. It had earned its preservation.

"How many manuscripts have you digitized?" Claire asked. Her tone was professional, interested, asking the right questions. She'd adopted the manner of a business partner evaluating a potential collaboration — engaged but not too engaged.

"Fourteen thousand pages in the last three years." Hrubý led them toward a second workstation. "Mostly Middle Eastern and Central Asian materials. We focus on regions where the originals are at risk — war, political instability, climate damage, institutional neglect. The digitization creates a permanent record. Even if the original is destroyed, the knowledge survives."

He showed them the second floor — project management, the map room, the small conference room where grants were planned and budgets were argued over.
